<div id="review_1316358267" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1316358267" />
    <a title="Anna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14941179-anna"><img alt="Anna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1353981100p2/14941179.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1316358267?book_show_action=true">Jun 24, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anna" href="/user/show/14941179-anna">Anna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140282450" href="/book/show/1815460.The_Disorderly_Knights">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14941179-anna?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14941179-anna?shelf=tudors">tudors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14941179-anna?shelf=scottish">scottish</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1316358267" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15981333264775309040">The Lymond Chronicles demand a fair amount of effort from the reader, as the huge cast and byzantine political manouverings can be a lot to keep up with. Yet the rewards are considerable. Dunnett provides the most audacious action set- pieces (including the best duel I’ve ever read in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/112077.The_Game_of_Kings__The_Lymond_Chronicles___1_' title='The Game of Kings (The Lymond Chronicles, #1) by Dorothy Dunnett' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Game of Kings</a>), breathtakingly exciting plot twists, as well as extremely witty dialogue and a brilliant sense of time and place. This third novel, though, took me some while to get into and had what I consider</span>
  <span id="freeText15981333264775309040" style="display:none">The Lymond Chronicles demand a fair amount of effort from the reader, as the huge cast and byzantine political manouverings can be a lot to keep up with. Yet the rewards are considerable. Dunnett provides the most audacious action set- pieces (including the best duel I’ve ever read in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/112077.The_Game_of_Kings__The_Lymond_Chronicles___1_" title="The Game of Kings (The Lymond Chronicles, #1) by Dorothy Dunnett" rel="nofollow noopener">The Game of Kings</a>), breathtakingly exciting plot twists, as well as extremely witty dialogue and a brilliant sense of time and place. This third novel, though, took me some while to get into and had what I consider to be significant flaws. I found Lymond more trying than usual. His string of oblique and sarcastic statements, coupled with near omniscience and omnipotence, were similar to the previous two chronicles. In ‘The Disorderly Knights’, however, he is more inclined to punch teenage girls, which I object to in the strongest possible terms. This made it harder than ever to swallow all the commentary on his apparent charisma and success with women. Every character other than his mother and brother gives the clear impression of being in love with him, while periodically also wanting to murder him. Such inhuman charm suggests he’s some kind of fae. My favourite assessment of him in the whole book:<br /><br /><blockquote>"He’s a tongue, Mr Crawford has, hasn’t he?” said Chester Herald in a pleased voice. “We found that out in France. A proper lad. And what he got up to!”<br />“You should see what he gets up to here,” said Jerott, bored.</blockquote><br /><br />I must also note that Dunnett’s sex scenes invariably contain hilariously purple prose, for example:<br /><br /><blockquote>When, late at night, the shadow darkened the starlight behind her silent shrouds, and the door, whispering, admitted a deeper shadow, soft-footed and deft, it found her already perfect as a flower brought to its full-breathing height.</blockquote><br /><br />After a slow start, I did get carried away by the plot, in which Lymond decides he wants his own private army. The twist in the last third of the book is excellently done. While the action was as thrilling and impressive as ever, I can only give the novel three stars overall because I found the role of Joleta so thoroughly distasteful. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[This beautiful sixteen year old girl, with her ‘apricot’ coloured hair is at various times shown as an innocent child, a ‘bitch’ (several people use the epithet), a manipulative seductress, a nymphomaniac, a sadist, a victim of incestuous child abuse, and a murdered corpse. At no point does she seem like a believable person and everyone except Jerott treats her appallingly. Lymond is rude and violent, then sleeps with her under incredibly dubious circumstances. All the female characters find her tedious and spoiled, without apparently wondering why she’s like this. It’s mostly left to the reader’s imagination what Gabriel did to her in the past, but it was clearly horrific, and in the end he murders both her and her (their?!) unborn child. It surprises me to find a female author treating a female character in this fashion. I refuse to believe that the plot required Joleta to suffer as she did, without ever being presented as sympathetic.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />On the other hand, I thought her brother Gabriel was handled much better. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[It was very satisfying when he turned out to be the true villain of the piece, although the depths of his villainy were unnecessarily hyperbolic. Did he need to be Joleta’s abuser? I think not. Overall, though, he worked very well as a dark mirror of Lymond. Both of them are charismatic and machiavellian blond men who seem too good to be true. In Gabriel’s case, his facade of piety apparently conceals absolute evil. In Lymond’s case, he can’t be bothered with a mask of politeness and would rather irritate everyone with his insouciant competence. The climactic fight between Gabriel and Lymond in St Giles’ Church is a delightful slice of pure melodrama, particularly satisfying as I regularly walk past the place. Other especially dramatic moments include Will Scott’s death (which was genuinely moving), the whipping scene, and the powder magazine fire. Dunnett is very good at fires, actually. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />It might be a while before I read the next in the Lymond Chronicles as they are rich, intermittently indigestible fare. While Dunnett’s plots and action scenes are exemplary, the romance elements range from the absurd to the truly horrifying. Lymond himself can try your patience with his shenanigans; I have every sympathy for his long-suffering and sardonic mother Sybilla.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15981333264775309040" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3210464147" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3210464147" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1074924-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1579822923p2/1074924.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3210464147?book_show_action=true">Apr 19, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/1074924-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/52436655-the-disorderly-knights">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3210464147" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14201112741864284226">That bloody beautiful ending! I mean ... did you SEE that? That was, like, monumental. Just when you thought this one thing was happening, something else happened and made me realize, nope, that ain't what's happenin'. THIS is what's happening ... FOR NOW, anyway, because GUESS WHAT? That's not going to happen either because something else a whole HELL of a lot bigger and darker is going to happen. It's like Dunnett was holding me by the scruff of my neck and jerking me from one astounding surpr</span>
  <span id="freeText14201112741864284226" style="display:none">That bloody beautiful ending! I mean ... did you SEE that? That was, like, monumental. Just when you thought this one thing was happening, something else happened and made me realize, nope, that ain't what's happenin'. THIS is what's happening ... FOR NOW, anyway, because GUESS WHAT? That's not going to happen either because something else a whole HELL of a lot bigger and darker is going to happen. It's like Dunnett was holding me by the scruff of my neck and jerking me from one astounding surprise to another. And then finally, the thing that DID happen started to happen, at which point I was all like YO what the fuck just happened?<br /><br />Amazing story. It did not end the way I wanted it to end, but then again it ended exactly the way it needed to end. I told myself--when I felt bogged down in the middle parts--that I was going to take a break from this series for a bit, try to read things that don't hurt my puny brain so much, but now OF COURSE I WANT TO READ MORE!<br /><br />Dunnett has the most cinematic voice in all of literature. There was not a detail lacking in any of the fights or flights; I was able to picture everything with rare clarity.<br /><br />What Dunnett lacks, however, is moderation of historical trivia. There were times I wanted to put this book down because, oh my God is she really still going on and on about some historical occasion or another seemingly without end? The whatever it was called ... the airing of grievances in Something Stank ... I'm sure that what she portrayed there was perfectly and indisputably accurate, but I found no value in it. I know it presented us with details that we were going to need in order to understand what happens later, but I contend there MUST have been a gentler, less tedious way to present all that information to us. For someone like me who is a molasses-slow reader and who often reads sentences twice to make sure he understands (or at least pretends to understand) what is happening, these sojourns through Dunnett's maze of vast and tedious historical knowledge were mildly excruciating.<br /><br />But alas, I loved it. Those tedious parts are almost forgotten now. I'm only remembering them in order to air my own grievances. As soon as I'm finished ranting about them, those scenes that I wish had been edited out will vanish and I'll be left only with my rumination on a bloody brilliant story.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14201112741864284226" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1310986469" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1310986469" />
    <a title="Dillwynia Peter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8394997-dillwynia-peter"><img alt="Dillwynia Peter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1332831047p2/8394997.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1310986469?book_show_action=true">Jun 17, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dillwynia Peter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dillwynia Peter" href="/user/show/8394997-dillwynia-peter">Dillwynia Peter</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1310986469" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15749571470775616970">I'm a little conflicted about this book in the Lymond series. I loved much of it in retrospect but there were a good 200 pages of this 590 tiny print book that bored me to tears. I'm hoping it was just me being tired rather than the novel itself.<br/><br/>At heart this book is about a psychopath, except it will take you close to 90% before you discover this & then all the previous actions & comments make perfect sense. Before then, Lymond is truly annoying & a shit at baiting an honest and honourable man.</span>
  <span id="freeText15749571470775616970" style="display:none">I'm a little conflicted about this book in the Lymond series. I loved much of it in retrospect but there were a good 200 pages of this 590 tiny print book that bored me to tears. I'm hoping it was just me being tired rather than the novel itself.<br /><br />At heart this book is about a psychopath, except it will take you close to 90% before you discover this &amp; then all the previous actions &amp; comments make perfect sense. Before then, Lymond is truly annoying &amp; a shit at baiting an honest and honourable man. There will be incidents that seem to be overexplained and narrated, which once again, at the end prove necessary for you to believe in why our "man" is a psychopath.<br /><br />The historical aspects are beautifully done as always by Dunnett: the insidious infighting of the Spanish &amp; French that weakens and destroys the St John Templars of Malta, the unrest within the Arabic world as the Ottomans gain power &amp; territory, and the intrigue that surrounds the Scottish court as this small country is being played with master players of the French &amp; English royalty - are juggled and captured in a way that entertains AND instructs.<br /><br />So in construction we have the scene set in the shorter 1st two parts, along with a big slab of history; in the final longest Part, we are reading a mystery. For those coming after me, this is really really important. Stick with it &amp; enjoy the fun at the end. In someways it felt like two separate novels with a common link; so along with all the mystery, the historical references and me being rather overworked as I read this, it was all too confusing &amp; frustrating. However, this lazy weekend has finalised the read &amp; I now respect the mastery that I have come to know Dunnett. I'm really keen to get the next one, as from the title I suspect it is a carry on from the latter events in this novel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15749571470775616970" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1816870684" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1816870684" />
    <a title="Lorena Blanco" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31467824-lorena-blanco"><img alt="Lorena Blanco" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1400345592p2/31467824.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1816870684?book_show_action=true">Nov 28, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lorena Blanco" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lorena Blanco" href="/user/show/31467824-lorena-blanco">Lorena Blanco</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/9527788-the-disorderly-knights">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1816870684" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14951973340860145348">such a roller coster this book. The first third of it is fast paced, brilliantly described, luxurious imagery. We get to see Lymond growing up, maturing, and trying to make up for his past errors. The beautiful landscape of Malta, Tripoli, Gozo is the setting for the meeting with an astonishing new character, Sir Graham Red Mallett, and you better remember his name.<br/>Back to Scotland, where Lymond engages in building his own superb mega elite army, and the spyral down begins again. So much. You ge</span>
  <span id="freeText14951973340860145348" style="display:none">such a roller coster this book. The first third of it is fast paced, brilliantly described, luxurious imagery. We get to see Lymond growing up, maturing, and trying to make up for his past errors. The beautiful landscape of Malta, Tripoli, Gozo is the setting for the meeting with an astonishing new character, Sir Graham Red Mallett, and you better remember his name.<br />Back to Scotland, where Lymond engages in building his own superb mega elite army, and the spyral down begins again. So much. You get to really hate this part. I mean HATE IT. You even get to hate Lymond. I don't think I will able to reconcile with this new layer of him, the man is brilliant, excel at everything, but he's so flawed at the same time. And maybe his virtousism is his worst enemy. Up until 80% questions pile up until it's unbearable. But then the last 20% is full speed, free fall, you cry, you scream, you hit your head against the wall. And this huge, heart breaking ending, which sets in motion events for the next book. Maybe I should note that in a rare treat, I got to guess the ending, and even then, it was magnificent!</span>
  <a data-text-id="14951973340860145348" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1816191136" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1816191136" />
    <a title="Giki" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/61057135-giki"><img alt="Giki" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1488989523p2/61057135.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1816191136?book_show_action=true">Dec 12, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Giki" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Giki" href="/user/show/61057135-giki">Giki</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/61057135-giki?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/61057135-giki?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1816191136" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8279400120972577012">The third in the Lymond chronicles series. If you have got this far then you have probably read and enjoyed the first two books. You will be just as frustrated, disappointed, shocked and annoyed by this glorious book as you were by the previous 2. I kind of despise myself for being so consumed by these books, but like the addict I am, I dived straight into the next one after finishing this.<br/><br/>The book opens in raucous style with Will Scott marrying his auntie in the middle of a border raid. After F</span>
  <span id="freeText8279400120972577012" style="display:none">The third in the Lymond chronicles series. If you have got this far then you have probably read and enjoyed the first two books. You will be just as frustrated, disappointed, shocked and annoyed by this glorious book as you were by the previous 2. I kind of despise myself for being so consumed by these books, but like the addict I am, I dived straight into the next one after finishing this.<br /><br />The book opens in raucous style with Will Scott marrying his auntie in the middle of a border raid. After France, for political reasons best instantly forgotten, Lymond is required to travel to Malta to assist the knights of St. John in the defense of the Island against the Turks. His decision to go is definitely not in any way influenced the fact that a certain Oonagh O Dwyer has taken up residence on the nearby Island of Gozo.<br /><br />Throughout the early part of the book I feel Lymond is like a frustrating shadow – always somewhere else – his story told, as usual, though someone else eyes, but we seem more distant from the action and the sparkling wit than in previous books. The language has changed also, gone are the over complex, obscure, multilingual references in everything Lymond says. Perhaps he learned in France, just how irritating everyone else found that. I kind of miss it, it was part of the rhythm of the reading, you can skim over, ignore what you don't understand, or you can rub away and reveal the gems underneath.<br />Lymond has changed too, he is sober for one thing, and appears competent and in control, at the peak of his powers. Trying to build for the future. Taking responsibility for his own actions and the consequences they might have. He seems to have leaned from O'Liamroe's painfully spelled out lesson on leadership at the end of the last book.<br /><br />And then he does something. Something so despicable, unexpectedly cruel, selfish and ill-timed that there can be no going back, there can surely be no redemption now. I felt nauseous with the force of it, as if I, in admiring so much this book and being so engrossed in the reading of it, was somehow partly culpable for the shocking transgression. The author's skill is such that I hardly notice the writing, the way she leads us into this trap, this is fiction after all, but the emotion is visceral.<br /><br />This book is wonderfully well written, intelligent, shocking and irreverent – the author is in complete control at all times. She has a wonderful talent for historical description, her descriptions of the people and places of 16th-century Scotland, that I vaguely remember from my dull dry high school history lessons, are wonderfully evocative and alive with atmosphere. I know how it feels now, to stumble around the night time streets of old Edinburgh, and what dangers may lurk around every corner.<br /><br />Read this book – there is good stuff here. Expect to work for it, but it will be worth it in the end.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8279400120972577012" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_315446874" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/315446874" />
    <a title="sarah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/81271-sarah"><img alt="sarah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1182137365p2/81271.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/315446874?book_show_action=true">Apr 19, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="sarah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="sarah" href="/user/show/81271-sarah">sarah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer315446874" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer435560933987408949">I deducted a star just because <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[a lot of the conflict here turned on the supposed chastity or lack thereof of a female character, which always makes me uncomfortable no matter how villainous that character might be or how consistent it might be with the time period the book depicts. <a class='jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction'>(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> That said, nobody could ever accuse Dunnett of not loving all her other magnificent female characters (Oonagh! Sybilla! Kate and Philippa Freaking Somerville!!!) so I can overcome that d</span>
  <span id="freeText435560933987408949" style="display:none">I deducted a star just because <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[a lot of the conflict here turned on the supposed chastity or lack thereof of a female character, which always makes me uncomfortable no matter how villainous that character might be or how consistent it might be with the time period the book depicts. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> That said, nobody could ever accuse Dunnett of not loving all her other magnificent female characters (Oonagh! Sybilla! Kate and Philippa Freaking Somerville!!!) so I can overcome that distaste.<br /><br />Everything else though, was pretty fantastic--the geographical sweep is enormous and the action set-pieces are pretty universally thrilling. With all those set-pieces the story feels pretty episodic right up until the end when you realize how all of the episodes tie together <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[into a cohesive, insanely crafty plot orchestrated by a single character--who on the one hand is revealed to be so over-the-top mustache-twirlingly evil that it's almost ridiculous but on the other hand HAD ME COMPLETELY FOOLED THE ENTIRE TIME. Well played, Lady Dunnett. Well played. Not to mention, no matter how cliched, how can you help but love drawing-room-mystery scenes where everyone gathers together and hears the full case laid out against the perpetrator and climactic hero-vs-villain sword-battle finales? <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Additionally, in a lot of other books where the prose is ornate and inventive and marvelous, I find that the sheer craft of the writing can sometimes hold me at arm's length from the characters and the emotion of the story, but these books never do. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Yes, I cried when Will Scott died. And when Jerott stayed. And when Sybilla was handed her grandson and Philippa didn't understand that she was crying because she was thinking about the other one she had never seen. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> You're never hit over the head with anything, it just creeps up and quietly devastates you. God, this series. I'm already sad that someday I'll finish it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="435560933987408949" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('4OudUQrR0basJ+IiSJmrc666iuKKopo/i+3biG4KVsQwB/a3pRtL5zBJjxhSFMPtZ/SaGfbZo6HuP018Tg4AVw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ziJPaUpkE+vnCq64DTJ1dbIzde5qQDb9DvCTLGu4q7IeziSP5a6Juntkw4IXvx3re31lFRY7D2NrIgXYS7z9IQ==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

How to Speak Money is acclaimed writer John Lanchester’s entertaining and informative attempt to bridge the gap between the money people and the rest of us. With characteristic wit and candor (“wickedly funny” — Dwight Garner, New York Times), Lanchester shows how the world of finance and economics really works — from the terms and conditions of your personal checking account to the evasions of bankers appearing in front of Congress. How to Speak Money reveals how the language of money is often a tool to conceal and mislead; he explains hundreds of common economic terms, from GDP to the IMF, amortization to securitization to Each day thousands turn to her \'food bible\' for the most reliable recipes and advice. But before Stephanie Alexander penned a word for the emerging food media, let alone for The Cook\'s Companion, she had spent decades avidly documenting food experiences.\n\nShaped by her mother\'s dedication to good food and her father\'s love of reading, she trained as a librarian and all the while observed, assessed and re-created the dishes she loved. Her monthly university allowance rarely lasted more than a week – all spent on pan-fried flounder and chestnut Mont Blanc.\n\nShe was seduced over pain Poîlane while working as an au pair in Paris, and later over ackee and saltfish in London. In 1966, with no formal culinary training and a newborn baby, but brimming with confidence and sheer determination, she opened Jamaica House with her first husband. The personal toll was great and it was many years until she emerged on the restaurant scene again.\n\nStephanie\'s Restaurant would become part of Melbourne food folklore, permanently raising the bar for restaurant dining in Australia. At the time of its opening, in 1976, a salad to most people meant iceberg lettuce, no-one had heard of goat\'s cheese and ginger came in a tin. Over the next twenty-one years, in her quest for the sort of produce she had enjoyed while living and travelling in Europe, Stephanie championed small local suppliers or grew it herself.\n\nHer indefatigable determination and single-minded vision have influenced – and sometimes intimidated – a generation of chefs, cooks and diners. And now her Kitchen Garden Foundation is inspiring tens of thousands of primary school children across Australia to grow and cook their own food.\n\nA Cook\'s Life is a very personal account of one woman\'s uncompromising commitment to good food, and of how it shaped her life and changed the eating habits of a nation.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"14664703479014746190\" href=\"#\" During this period, accelerated change saw Britain’s turbulent entry into the modern age, and clothing reflected these transformations. Starting with the intimate perspective of clothing the self, Dress in the Age of Jane Austen moves outward through the social and cultural spheres of home, village, countryside, and cities, and into the wider national and global realms, exploring the varied ways people dressed to inhabit these environments. Jane Austen’s famously observant fictional writings, as well as her letters, provide the entry point for examining the Regency age’s rich complexity of fashion, dress, and textiles for men and women in their contemporary contexts.   Lavishly illustrated with paintings, drawings, historic garments, and fashion plates—including many previously unpublished images—this authoritative yet accessible book will help readers visualize the external selves of Austen’s immortal characters as clearly as she wrote of their internal ones.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/8361.Dorothy_Dunnett">About Dorothy Dunnett</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/8361.Dorothy_Dunnett"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1554147519p3/8361.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/8361.Dorothy_Dunnett">Dorothy Dunnett
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>

followers

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer14740354833254680171">Dorothy Dunnett OBE was a Scottish historical novelist. She is best known for her six-part series about Francis Crawford of Lymond, <i>The Lymond Chronicles</i>, which she followed with the eight-part prequel <i>The House of Niccolò</i>. She also wrote a novel about the real Macbeth called <i>King Hereafter</i> and a series of mystery novels centered on Johnson Johnson, a portrait painter/spy.<br/><br/><i>Her New York times obitua</i></span>
  <span id="freeText14740354833254680171" style="display:none">Dorothy Dunnett OBE was a Scottish historical novelist. She is best known for her six-part series about Francis Crawford of Lymond, <i>The Lymond Chronicles</i>, which she followed with the eight-part prequel <i>The House of Niccolò</i>.
